<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of Bayard Rustin" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1146" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/170px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/255px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/340px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Social Democrats USA was headed by National Chairman <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Social Democrats, USA</b> (<b>SDUSA</b>) is an association of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_left" title="American left">U.S.</a> <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democrats</a>, that had been called the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist Party of America</a> (SP) until the 1972 convention where it changed its name to SDUSA to clarify its objectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Socialist Party had stopped running independent candidates for president of the U.S., and consequently the name "party" had confused the public. Replacing the name "socialist" with "social democrat", SDUSA clarified its vision to Americans who confused <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">socialism</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_Communism" title="Soviet Communism">Soviet Communism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup> which SDUSA opposed. In response, former SPA Co-Chairman Michael Harrington resigned from SDUSA in 1973 and founded the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Organizing_Committee" title="Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee">Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee</a>, which criticized SDUSA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Communism" title="Anti-Communism">anti-Communism</a> and which welcomed the middle-class movements associated with the unsuccessful presidential campaign of <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodworth147-2">[2]</a></sup> SDUSA members opposed McGovern's politics; a few of them helped to start the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_for_a_Democratic_Majority" title="Coalition for a Democratic Majority">Coalition for a Democratic Majority</a>, and such members have been called <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_%22Scoop%22_Jackson" title='Henry "Scoop" Jackson'>"Scoop" Jackson</a></span> Democrats or <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neoconservatives</a> (or both).</p>
<p>SDUSA's members had been active in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a>, which had been led <a href="/wiki/African-American_Civil_Rights_Movement_(1896%E2%80%931954)#The_Labor_movement_and_civil_rights" title="African-American Civil Rights Movement (1896–1954)">since the 1940s</a> by <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph#Civil_rights_leader" title="A. Philip Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</a>. SDUSA's leaders had organized the 1963 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/March_on_Washington" title="March on Washington">March on Washington</a>, during which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> delivered his "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech. Under the leadership of Randolph and <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a>, SDUSA championed Rustin's <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin#From_Protest_to_Politics" title="Bayard Rustin">emphasis on economic inequality</a> as the most important issue facing African-Americans after the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>. SDUSA's efforts to reduce economic inequality led to a focus on labor unions and economic policy, and SDUSA members were active in the <a href="/wiki/AFL%E2%80%93CIO" title="AFL–CIO">AFL–CIO</a> confederation as well as in individual unions, especially the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers" title="American Federation of Teachers">American Federation of Teachers</a>.</p>
<p>SDUSA's electoral strategy ("realignment") intended to organize labor unions, civil rights organizations, and other constituencies into a coalition that would transform the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> into a social-democratic party. The realignment strategy emphasized working with unions and especially the <a href="/wiki/AFL%E2%80%93CIO" title="AFL–CIO">AFL–CIO</a>, putting an emphasis on economic issues that would unite working-class voters. SDUSA opposed the <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/New_Politics" title="New Politics">New Politics</a> of Senator <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a>, which had lost all states but Massachusetts to Richard Nixon at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1972" title="United States presidential election, 1972">1972 election</a>, when Americans voted for a Democratic House of Representatives in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1972" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1972">House elections</a>. While SDUSA had endorsed McGovern, it had adopted resolutions criticizing the New Politics for having made criticisms of labor unions and working-class Americans and for its advocacy of an immediate and unconditional U.S. withdrawal from Vietnam.</p>
<p>SDUSA's organizational activities included sponsoring discussions and issuing position papers; it was known mainly because of its members' activities in other organizations. SDUSA included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">civil-rights activists</a> and leaders of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_labor_movement" title="American labor movement">labor unions</a>, such as Bayard Rustin, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Hill" title="Norman Hill">Norman Hill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a> of the <a href="/wiki/AFL%E2%80%93CIO" title="AFL–CIO">AFL–CIO</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Feldman" title="Sandra Feldman">Sandra Feldman</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rachelle_Horowitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rachelle Horowitz (page does not exist)">Rachelle Horowitz</a> of the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers" title="American Federation of Teachers">American Federation of Teachers</a>. SDUSA's Tom Kahn organized the AFL-CIO's support of <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Poland's Solidarity</a>, an independent labor-union that challenged communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis32_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis32-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Penn_Kemble" title="Penn Kemble">Penn Kemble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gershman" title="Carl Gershman">Carl Gershman</a> cooperated with <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> and Democratic administrations on <a href="/wiki/Democracy_promotion" title="Democracy promotion">democracy promotion</a>, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan administration</a>. Other members included the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Sidney Hook</a>. SDUSA ceased operations in 2005, following the death of Penn Kemble. In 2008–2009 two small organizations emerged, each proclaiming itself to be the successor to SDUSA.</p>
<p>SDUSA's politics were criticized by former Socialist Party Chairman <a href="/wiki/Michael_Harrington" title="Michael Harrington">Michael Harrington</a>, who in 1972 announced that he favored an immediate pull-out of US forces from Vietnam (without requiring any guarantees); after losing all votes at the 1972 convention that changed the Socialist Party to SDUSA, Harrington resigned in 1973 and formed his <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Organizing_Committee" title="Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee">Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee</a> (DSOC), which welcomed the New Politics and middle-class leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodworth147-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington.2C_Fragments_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington.2C_Fragments-4">[4]</a></sup> The 1972 changing of the name of the Socialist Party of America to SDUSA and the 1973 formation of DSOC represented a split in the <a href="/wiki/American_Left" title="American Left">U.S. socialist movement</a>.</p>
<p>Some members of SDUSA have been called "right-wing social-democrats",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaisse91_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaisse91-5">[5]</a></sup> a taunt according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ben_Wattenberg" title="Ben Wattenberg">Ben Wattenberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wattenberg_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wattenberg-6">[6]</a></sup> SUDSA members supported the free labor-union of Poland, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solidarnosc" title="Solidarnosc">Solidarity</a> (<i>Solidarność</i>), with Tom Kahn working for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> and later Carl Gershman working for the <a href="/wiki/National_Endowment_for_Democracy" title="National Endowment for Democracy">National Endowment for Democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Domber_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Domber-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thiel_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thiel-10">[10]</a></sup> Their support of Solidarity was criticized by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carter_Administration" title="Carter Administration">Carter Administration</a>, the Soviet Union, and other supporters of <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1962%E2%80%9379)#.27.27D.C3.A9tente.27.27_and_changing_alliance" title="Cold War (1962–79)"><i>Détente</i></a>. SDUSA members (like the AFL-CIO and at Solidarity's request) supported using economic aid to Poland's Communist government as a bargaining chip to help Solidarity, while neoconservatives and "hard-line" conservatives opposed such aid in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnPodhoretz_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-KahnPodhoretz-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-CG11-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnDoubleStandards_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-KahnDoubleStandards-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>SDUSA leaders Penn Kemble and Bayard Rustin and former SDUSA-member Joshua Muravchik were called "second-generation neoconservatives" by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justin_Vaisse" title="Justin Vaisse">Justin Vaisse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaisse91_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaisse91-5">[5]</a></sup> These leaders, along with Kahn, Horowitz and Gersham, are also regarded as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shachtmanites" title="Shachtmanites">Shachtmanites</a> by most other scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> SDUSA leader Penn Kemble rejected the neoconservative label and called himself a social democrat (even while dying in 2005).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> Muravchik (the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Muravchik#YPSL" title="Joshua Muravchik">youth leader</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> disputed the Shachtmanite label for his generation and has called himself a neoconservative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuravchikWeNeoconservatives_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-MuravchikWeNeoconservatives-19">[19]</a></sup> to the disappointment of his SDUSA associates who continue to identify with social democracy and to disagree with neoconservatism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuravchikComrades_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-MuravchikComrades-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeyersonDissent_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MeyersonDissent-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_Party_of_America">Socialist Party of America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Socialist Party of America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist Party of America</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Organizing_Committee" title="Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee">Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_the_USA" title="Socialist Party of the USA">Socialist Party of the USA</a></div>
<p>By the early 1970s,the Socialist Party was publicly associated with <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph" title="A. Philip Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">civil-rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">labor union</a> leader, and with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Harrington" title="Michael Harrington">Michael Harrington</a>, the author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Other_America" title="The Other America">The Other America</a></i>. Even before the 1972 convention, Michael Harrington had resigned as an Honorary Chairperson of the Socialist Party,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup> "because he was upset about the group’s failure to enthusiastically support George McGovern and because of its views on the Vietnam War."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In its 1972 Convention, the Socialist Party had two Co-Chairmen, <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_S._Zimmerman" title="Charles S. Zimmerman">Charles S. Zimmerman</a> (of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Ladies_Garment_Workers%27_Union" title="International Ladies Garment Workers' Union">International Ladies Garment Workers' Union</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorin149_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorin149-24">[24]</a></sup> ILGWU) and a First National Vice Chairman, James S. Glaser, who were re-elected by acclamation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup> In his opening speech to the Convention, Co-Chairman Bayard Rustin called for SDUSA to organize against the "reactionary policies of the Nixon Administration"; Rustin also criticized the "irresponsibility and élitism of the 'New Politics' liberals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The party changed its name to "Social Democrats, USA" by a vote of 73 to 34.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup> Changing the name of the Socialist Party to "Social Democrats USA" was intended to be "realistic": the intention was to respond to the end of the running of actual Socialist Party candidates for office, to respond to the confusions of Americans. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> observed that the Socialist Party had last sponsored a <a href="/wiki/Darlington_Hoopes" title="Darlington Hoopes">Darlington Hoopes</a>, as candidate for president, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1956" title="U.S. presidential election, 1956">1956</a>, who received only 2,121 votes, which were cast in only six states. Because the Socialist Party no longer sponsored party candidates in elections, continued use of the name "party" was "misleading" and hindered the recruiting of activists who participated in the Democratic Party, according to the majority report. The name "Socialist" was replaced by "Social Democrats" because many American associated the word "<a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">socialism</a>" with Soviet communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup> Moreover, the organization sought to distinguish itself from two small Marxist parties, the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Workers_Party_(United_States)" title="Socialist Workers Party (United States)">Socialist Workers Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Labor_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Labor Party of America">Socialist Labor Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT27_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT27-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1972 convention, the majority ("Unity Caucus") won every vote, by a ratio of two to one. The Convention elected a national committee of 33 members, with 22 seats for the majority caucus, eight seats for the "Coalition Caucus" of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Harrington" title="Michael Harrington">Michael Harrington</a>, two for the left-wing "Debs Caucus", and one for the "independent" <a href="/wiki/Samuel_H._Friedman" title="Samuel H. Friedman">Samuel H. Friedman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT73_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT73-26">[26]</a></sup> Friedman and the minority caucuses had opposed the name change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The convention voted on and adopted proposals for its program by a two-one vote. On foreign policy, the program called for "firmness toward Communist aggression". However, on the Vietnam War, the program opposed "any efforts to bomb Hanoi into submission"; instead, it endorsed negotiating a peace agreement, which should protect Communist political cadres in South Vietnam from further military or police reprisals. Harrington's proposal for a <a href="/wiki/Ceasefire" title="Ceasefire">ceasefire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immediate_withdrawal" title="Immediate withdrawal">immediate withdrawal</a> of U.S. forces was defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT73_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT73-26">[26]</a></sup> Harrington complained that, after its convention, the Socialist Party had endorsed <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a> only with a statement loaded with "constructive criticism" and that it had not mobilized enough support for McGovern. The majority caucus's <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arch_Puddington&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arch Puddington (page does not exist)">Arch Puddington</a> replied that the California branch was especially active in supporting McGovern, while the New York branch were focusing on a congressional race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT27_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT27-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the Socialist Party changed its name to Social Democrats, USA, <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> became its public spokesman. According to Rustin, SDUSA aimed to transform the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> into a social-democratic party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT74_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT74-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Some months after the convention, Michael Harrington resigned his membership in SDUSA; he and some of his supporters from the Coalition Caucus soon formed the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Organizing_Committee" title="Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee">Democratic Socialist Organizing Committee</a> (DSOC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Many members of the Debs caucus resigned from SDUSA, and some of them formed the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_USA" title="Socialist Party USA">Socialist Party USA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The changing of the name of the Socialist Party of America to SDUSA and the 1973 formation of DSOC and the SPUSA represented a split in the <a href="/wiki/American_Left" title="American Left">U.S. socialist movement</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1972.svg"><img alt="Map of the United States, showing Nixon's victories in 49 states (red) over McGovern." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="1020" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ElectoralCollege1972.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1972.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ElectoralCollege1972.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1972.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ElectoralCollege1972.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1972.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1972.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
SDUSA opposed the politics of George McGovern, whose 1972 presidential campaign lost 49 of 50 states to Richard Nixon.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:93_us_house_membership.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="506" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/93_us_house_membership.png/300px-93_us_house_membership.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/93_us_house_membership.png/450px-93_us_house_membership.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/93_us_house_membership.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</td>

</td>
<td>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> up to 60% Democratic</div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In domestic politics, the SDUSA leadership emphasized the role of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_labor_movement" title="American labor movement">American labor movement</a> in advancing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_justice" title="Economic justice">economic justice</a>. The domestic program followed the recommendations of Rustin's article "From Protest to Politics". In it, Rustin analyzed the changing economy and its implications for African Americans. Rustin wrote that the rise of automation would reduce the demand for low-skill high-paying jobs, which would jeopardize the position of the urban Black working-class, particularly in the northern US. The needs of the Black community demanded a shift in political strategy, where Blacks would need to strengthen their political alliance with mostly white unions and other organizations (churches, synagogues, etc.) to pursue a common economic agenda. It was time to move from protest to politics, wrote Rustin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinFPTP_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-RustinFPTP-31">[31]</a></sup> A particular danger facing the Black community was the chimera of <a href="/wiki/Identity_politics" title="Identity politics">identity politics</a>, particularly the rise of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_power" title="Black power">Black power</a>" which Rustin dismissed as a fantasy of middle-class African-Americans that repeated the political and moral errors of previous <a href="/wiki/Black_nationalism" title="Black nationalism">black nationalists</a>, while alienating the white allies needed by the Black community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinSeparatism_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-RustinSeparatism-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>SDUSA documents had similar criticisms of the agendas advanced by middle-class activists increasing their role in the Democratic Party. SDUSA members stated concerns about an exaggerated role of "middle-class" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_U.S._involvement_in_the_Vietnam_War" title="Opposition to the U.S. involvement in the Vietnam War">peace activists</a> in the Democratic Party, particularly associated with the "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=New_Politics_(1968)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="New Politics (1968) (page does not exist)">New Politics</a>" of Senator <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern_presidential_campaign,_1972" title="George McGovern presidential campaign, 1972">presidential candidacy</a> was viewed as an ongoing disaster for the Democratic Party and for the USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodworth147-2">[2]</a></sup> In electoral politics, SDUSA aimed to transform the Democratic Party into a social democratic party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT74_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT74-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In foreign policy, most of the founding SDUSA leadership called for an immediate cessation of the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Rolling_Thunder" title="Operation Rolling Thunder">bombing</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a>. They demanded a negotiated peace treaty to end the Vietnam War. However, the majority opposed a unilateral withdrawal of US forces from Vietnam, suggesting that such a withdrawal would lead to an annihilation of the free labor-unions and of the political opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gershman_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gershman-34">[34]</a></sup> After the withdrawal of U.S. forces from Vietnam and the victory of the Vietnamese Communists, SDUSA supported humanitarian assistance to refugees and condemned Senator McGovern for his failure to support such assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_activities">Organizational activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Organizational activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>SDUSA was governed by biannual conventions which invited the participation of interested observers. These gatherings featured discussions and debates over proposed resolutions, some of which were adopted as organizational statements. The group frequently made use of outside speakers at these events: non-SDUSA intellectuals ranged from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoconservative" title="Neoconservative">neoconservatives</a> like <a href="/wiki/Jeane_Kirkpatrick" title="Jeane Kirkpatrick">Jeane Kirkpatrick</a> on the right to democratic socialists like <a href="/wiki/Paul_Berman" title="Paul Berman">Paul Berman</a> on the left; similarly, a range of academic, political, and labor-union leaders were invited. These meetings also functioned as reunions for political activists and intellectuals, some of whom worked together for decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeyersonDissent_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-MeyersonDissent-21">[21]</a></sup> SDUSA also published a newsletter and occasional position papers.</p>
<p>SDUSA issued statements supporting labor unions and workers' interests at home and overseas. It supported the existence of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and the Israeli labor movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Challenge_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Challenge-37">[37]</a></sup> It opposed many of the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">G. W. Bush administration<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span></a> domestic policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muravchik_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-muravchik-38">[38]</a></sup> From 1979–1989, SDUSA members were organized to support of <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)"><i>Solidarnosc</i> (Solidarity), the independent labor-union of Poland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahler_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahler-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The organization also attempted to exert influence through endorsements of Presidential candidates. The group's 1976 National Convention, held in New York City, formally endorsed the Democratic ticket of <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Mondale" title="Walter Mondale">Walter Mondale</a> and pledged the group to "work enthusiastically" for the election of the pair in November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The organization took a less assertive approach during the divisive 1980 campaign, marked as it was by a heated primary challenge to President Carter by Senator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Kennedy" title="Edward Kennedy">Edward Kennedy</a>; SDUSA chose not to hold its biannual convention until after the termination of the fall campaign. The election of conservative <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> was chalked up to the failure of the Democrats to "appeal to their traditional working class constituency."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early in 1980 long-time National Director <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gershman" title="Carl Gershman">Carl Gershman</a> resigned his position, to be replaced by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rita_Freedman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rita Freedman (page does not exist)">Rita Freedman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewDir_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewDir-42">[42]</a></sup> Freedman previously had served as organizer and chair of SDUSA's key New York local.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewDir_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewDir-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dues in Social Democrats, USA were paid annually in advance, with members receiving a copy of the organization's official organ, the tabloid-sized newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/New_America_(newspaper)" title="New America (newspaper)">New America</a>.</i> The dues rate was $25 per year in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_activities">Member activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Member activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Small organizations associated with the Debs–Thomas Socialist Party have served as schools for the leadership of social-movement organizations, including the civil-rights movement and the sixties radicalism. These organizations are now chiefly remembered because of their members' leadership of large organizations that directly influenced USA and international politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> After 1960 the party also functioned "as an educational organization" and "a caucus of policy advocates on the left wing of the Democratic Party".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Similarly, SDUSA was known mainly because of the activities of its members, many of whom publicly identified themselves as members of SDUSA. Members of SDUSA have served as officers for governmental, private, and not-for-profit organizations. <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph" title="A. Philip Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Hill" title="Norman Hill">Norman Hill</a> were leaders of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Feldman" title="Sandra Feldman">Sandra Feldman</a>, and Rachelle Horowitz were officers of labor unions. Carl Gershman and <a href="/wiki/Penn_Kemble" title="Penn Kemble">Penn Kemble</a> served in governmental and non-governmental organizations, particularly in foreign policy. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Sidney Hook</a> was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_intellectual" title="Public intellectual">public intellectual</a>.</p>
<p>Writing after the death of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ben_Wattenberg" title="Ben Wattenberg">Ben Wattenberg</a> commented that SDUSA as an "umbrella organization" associated with other <a href="/wiki/Letterhead_organization" title="Letterhead organization">letterhead organizations</a>; according to him, SDUSA members seemed to be</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"ingeniously trying to bury the Soviet Union in a blizzard of letterheads. It seemed that each of Tom's colleagues—Penn Kemble, Carl Gershman, Josh Muravchik and many more—ran a little organization, each with the same interlocking directorate listed on the stationery. Funny thing: The Letterhead Lieutenants did indeed churn up a blizzard, and the Soviet Union is no more.</p>
<p>I never did quite get all the organizational acronyms straight—YPSL, LID, SP, SDA, ISL—but the key words were "democratic", "labor", "young" and, until events redefined it away from their understanding, "socialist". Ultimately, the umbrella group became "Social Democrats, U.S.A", and Tom Kahn was a principal "theoretician".</p>
<p>They talked and wrote endlessly, mostly about communism and democracy, despising the former, adoring the latter. It is easy today to say "anti-communist" and "pro-democracy" in the same breath. But that is because American foreign policy eventually became just such a mixture, thanks in part to those "Yipsels" (Young People's Socialist League), with Tom Kahn as provocateur-at-large.</p>
<p>On the conservative side, foreign policy used to be <i>anti-communist,</i> but not very <i>pro-democracy.</i> And foreign policy liberal-style might be piously <i>pro-democracy,</i> but nervous about being <i>anti-communist.</i> Tom theorized that to be either, you had to be both.</p>
<p>It was tough for labor-liberal intellectuals to be "anti-communist" in the 1970s. It meant being taunted as "Cold Warriors" who saw "Commies under every bed" and being labeled as—the unkindest cut—"right-wingers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wattenberg_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wattenberg-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A._Philip_Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: A. Philip Randolph">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg"><img alt="Picture of A. Philip Randolph." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3702" data-file-width="2767" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg/170px-A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg/255px-A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg/340px-A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A._Philip_Randolph_1963_NYWTS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A. Philip Randolph was a visible member of the Socialist Party of Norman Thomas and then of Social Democrats, USA.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph" title="A. Philip Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</a></div>
<p>The long-time leader and intellectual architect of the civil rights movement, A. Philip Randolph was also a visible member of the Socialist Party of <a href="/wiki/Norman_Thomas" title="Norman Thomas">Norman Thomas</a>. He remained with the organization when it changed its name to SDUSA. Along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ILGWU" title="ILGWU">ILGWU</a> President <a href="/wiki/David_Dubinsky" title="David Dubinsky">David Dubinsky</a>, Randolph was honored at the 1976 SDUSA convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hook76_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hook76-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>A. Philip Randolph came to national attention as the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Brotherhood_of_Sleeping_Car_Porters" title="Brotherhood of Sleeping Car Porters">Brotherhood of Sleeping Car Porters</a>. Randolph proposed a march on Washington, D.C. to protest racial discrimination in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Armed_Forces" title="U.S. Armed Forces">U.S. Armed Forces</a>. Meeting with <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Presidency of Franklin D. Roosevelt">President Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>, Randolph respectfully, politely, but firmly told President Roosevelt that Negroes would march in the capital unless desegregation would occur. The planned march was canceled after President Roosevelt issued <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_8802" title="Executive Order 8802">Executive Order 8802</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fair_Employment_Act" title="Fair Employment Act">Fair Employment Act</a>), which banned discrimination in defense industries and federal agencies.</p>
<p>In 1942, an estimated 18,000 blacks gathered at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> to hear Randolph kick off a campaign against discrimination in the military, in war industries, in government agencies, and in labor unions.  Following the act, during the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Philadelphia_Transit_Strike&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Philadelphia Transit Strike (page does not exist)">Philadelphia Transit Strike</a> of 1944, the government backed African-American workers' striking to gain positions formerly limited to white employees.</p>
<p>In 1947, Randolph, along with colleague <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Grant_Reynolds_(civil_rights_leader)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Grant Reynolds (civil rights leader) (page does not exist)">Grant Reynolds</a>, renewed efforts to end discrimination in the armed services, forming the Committee Against <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_laws" title="Jim Crow laws">Jim Crow</a> in Military Service, later renamed the League for Non-Violent <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">Civil Disobedience</a>.  On July 26, 1948, President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> abolished <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> in the armed forces through <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_9981" title="Executive Order 9981">Executive Order 9981</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dol_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-dol-48">[48]</a></sup> Randolph was the nominal leader of the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom</a>, which was organized by <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> and his younger associates. At this march, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> delivered his "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech. Soon aferwords, the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> was passed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bayard_Rustin">Bayard Rustin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Bayard Rustin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2096" data-file-width="2728" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg/267px-Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg/401px-Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg/534px-Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bayard_Rustin_NYWTS_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rustin, 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a></div>
<p>Bayard Rustin was National Chairman of SDUSA. He also was President of the <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph_Institute" title="A. Philip Randolph Institute">A. Philip Randolph Institute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinGershman_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-RustinGershman-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rustin had had a long association with A. Philip Randolph and with pacifist movements. In 1956 Rustin advised <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> who was organizing the <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_bus_boycott" title="Montgomery bus boycott">Montgomery bus boycott</a>. According to Rustin, "I think it's fair to say that Dr. King's view of non-violent tactics was almost non-existent when the boycott began. In other words, Dr. King was permitting himself and his children and his home to be protected by guns." Rustin convinced King to abandon the armed protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The following year, Rustin and King began organizing the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">Southern Christian Leadership Conference</a> (SCLC).</p>
<p>Rustin and Randolph organized the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom</a> in 1963. On September 6, 1963 Rustin and Randolph appeared on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine as "the leaders" of the March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_protest_to_politics">From protest to politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: From protest to politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After passage of the 1964 <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act</a> and 1965 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act" title="Voting Rights Act">Voting Rights Act</a>, Rustin advocated closer ties between the civil rights movement and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and its base among the working class.</p>
<p>With the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHKahn_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-RHKahn-54">[54]</a></sup> Rustin wrote the 1965 article "From protest to politics",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FPTP_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FPTP-55">[55]</a></sup> which analyzed the changing economy and its implications for American Negroes. This article stated that the rise of automation would reduce the demand for low-skill high-paying jobs, which would jeopardize the position of the urban Negro working-class, particularly in the northern US. To pursue its economic agenda, the Negro community needed to shift political strategy, strengthening its political alliance with mostly white unions and other organizations (churches, synagogues, etc.). As its agenda shifted from civil rights to economic justice, the Negro community's tactics needed to shift from protest to politics, wrote Rustin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinFPTP_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-RustinFPTP-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>A particular danger facing the Negro community was the chimera of <a href="/wiki/Identity_politics" title="Identity politics">identity politics</a>, particularly the rise of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_power" title="Black power">Black power</a>", for which Rustin expressed contempt:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Wearing my hair Afro style, calling myself an Afro-American, and eating all the chitterlings I can find are not going to affect Congress."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKRustin_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-RKRustin-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rustin wrote that "Black power" repeated the moral errors of previous <a href="/wiki/Black_nationalism" title="Black nationalism">black nationalists</a>, while alienating the white allies needed by the Negro community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinSeparatism_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-RustinSeparatism-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_William_Julius_Wilson">Influence on William Julius Wilson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Influence on William Julius Wilson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/William_Julius_Wilson" title="William Julius Wilson">William Julius Wilson</a></div>
<p>Rustin's analysis was supported by the later research by William Julius Wilson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKRustin_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-RKRustin-56">[56]</a></sup> Wilson documented an increase in inequality within the Black community, following educated Blacks moving into white suburbs and following the decrease of demand for low-skill labor, as industry declined in the Northern USA. Such economic problems were not being addressed by a civil rights leadership focused on "<a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>", a policy benefiting the truly advantaged within the Black community. Wilson's criticism of the neglect of working-class and poor African Americans by civil rights organizations led to his being mistaken for a conservative, despite his having identified himself as a Rustin-style social democrat. Wilson has served on the advisory board of Social Democrats, USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_movement:_unions_and_social_democracy">Labor movement: unions and social democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Labor movement: unions and social democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Rustin increasingly worked to strengthen the labor movement, which he saw as the champion of empowerment for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> community and for economic justice for all Americans. He contributed to the labor movement's two sides, economic and political, through support of labor unions and social-democratic politics.</p>
<p>He was the founder and became the Director of the <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph_Institute" title="A. Philip Randolph Institute">A. Philip Randolph Institute</a>, which coordinated the AFL-CIO's work on civil rights and economic justice. He became a regular columnist for the AFL-CIO newspaper.</p>
<p>On the political side of the labor movement, Rustin increased his visibility as a leader of the American <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracy</a>. He was a founding National Co-Chairman of Social Democrats, USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT74_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT74-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights.2C_especially_ending_discrimination_against_gays">Human rights, especially ending discrimination against gays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Human rights, especially ending discrimination against gays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Throughout the 1970s and 1980s, Rustin worked as a <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Election_monitoring" title="Election monitoring">election monitor</a> for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>. He also testified on behalf of New York State's <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">Gay</a> Rights Bill. In 1986, he gave a speech "The new 'niggers' are gays," in which he asserted,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Today, blacks are no longer the litmus paper or the barometer of social change. Blacks are in every segment of society and there are laws that help to protect them from racial discrimination. The new "niggers" are gays.... It is in this sense that gay people are the new barometer for social change.... The question of social change should be framed with the most vulnerable group in mind: gay people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rustin also helped to write a report on peaceful means to end <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">Apartheid</a> (racial segregation) in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_Hill">Norman Hill</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Norman Hill">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norman_Hill" title="Norman Hill">Norman Hill</a></div>
<p>Norman Hill (born April 22, 1933 in <a href="/wiki/Summit,_New_Jersey" title="Summit, New Jersey">Summit, New Jersey</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>) is an influential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-American</a> administrator, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activist" title="Activist">activist</a> and labor leader.</p>
<p>Graduating in 1956, he was one of the first African-Americans to graduate from <a href="/wiki/Haverford_College" title="Haverford College">Haverford College</a>. Joining the civil rights movement and working in Chicago, Hill was an organizer for the Youth March for Integrated Schools, and then Secretary of Chicago Area Negro American Labor Council, and Staff Chairman of the Chicago March Conventions. In the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Racial_Equality" title="Congress of Racial Equality">Congress of Racial Equality</a> (CORE), Hill was first the East Coast Field Secretary and then National Program Director. He assisted <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> with organizing the 1963 March on Washington. As National Program Director of CORE, Hill coordinated the route 40 <a href="/wiki/Desegregation" title="Desegregation">desegregation</a> of restaurants, the Waldorf campaign, and illustrated the civil rights demonstration that took place at the <a href="/wiki/1964_Republican_National_Convention" title="1964 Republican National Convention">1964 Republican National Convention</a>.</p>
<p>From 1964 to 1967, Norman Hill served as the Legislative Representative and Civil Rights Liaison of the Industrial Union department of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a>. He was involved in the issue of raising minimum wage and the labor delegation on the <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">Selma to Montgomery marches</a> against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a> in politics and voting in the southern United States.</p>
<p>In 1967, Hill became active in the <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph_Institute" title="A. Philip Randolph Institute">A. Philip Randolph Institute</a>. He began as Associate Director, but later became Executive Director, and finally President. As Associate Director, Hill coordinated and organized the Memphis March in 1968, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King" title="Martin Luther King">Martin Luther King</a>’s assassination. In his career at the A. Philip Randolph Institute, Hill created over two hundred local chapters of this organization across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tom_Kahn">Tom Kahn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tom Kahn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a></div>
<p>Tom Kahn was a leader of SDUSA, who made notable contributions to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a> and to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labor_movement" title="Labor movement">labor movement</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kahn helped <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> organize the 1957 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prayer_Pilgrimage_to_Washington" title="Prayer Pilgrimage to Washington">Prayer Pilgrimage to Washington</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Youth_March_for_Integrated_Schools_(1958)" title="Youth March for Integrated Schools (1958)">1958</a> and <a href="/wiki/Youth_March_for_Integrated_Schools_(1959)" title="Youth March for Integrated Schools (1959)">1959 Youth March for Integrated Schools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> As a white student at historically black <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">Howard University</a>, Kahn and Norman Hill helped Rustin and <a href="/wiki/A._Philip_Randolph" title="A. Philip Randolph">A. Philip Randolph</a> to plan the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1963_March_on_Washington" title="1963 March on Washington">1963 March on Washington</a>, at which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> delivered his "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randolph_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randolph-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rustin_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rustin-64">[64]</a></sup> Kahn's role in the civil rights movement was discussed in <a class="external text" href="http://www.socialdemocratsusa.org/oldsite/Kahn.html" rel="nofollow">the eulogy by Rachelle Horowitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHKahn_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-RHKahn-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_of_Solidarity.2C_the_Polish_union">Support of Solidarity, the Polish union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Support of Solidarity, the Polish union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg/220px-Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg/330px-Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg/440px-Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borsen_gdansk_roads_to_freedom_exhibition_21p.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Polish labor-union's demand for legality were supported by Tom Kahn, who testified on behalf of the AFL-CIO to the US Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnPodhoretz_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-KahnPodhoretz-11">[11]</a></sup> The picture displays the <a href="/wiki/21_demands_of_MKS" title="21 demands of MKS">21 demands of Solidarity</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity (Polish trade union)</a></div>
<p>When he became an assistant to the President of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> from 1972–1986, Kahn developed an expertise in international affairs.</p>
<p>Kahn was deeply involved with supporting the Polish labor movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup> The trade union <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity</a> (<i>Solidarność</i>) began in 1980. The Soviet-backed communist regime headed by General <a href="/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski" title="Wojciech Jaruzelski">Wojciech Jaruzelski</a> declared <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> in December 1981. <a href="/wiki/Lane_Kirkland" title="Lane Kirkland">Lane Kirkland</a> appointed Kahn to organize the AFL-CIO's support of Solidarity. Politically, the AFL-CIO supported the <a href="/wiki/21_demands_of_MKS" title="21 demands of MKS">twenty-one demands of the Gdansk workers</a>, by lobbying to stop further U.S. loans to Poland unless those demands were met. Materially, the AFL-CIO established the Polish Workers Aid Fund, which raised almost $300,000 by 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup> These funds purchased printing presses, and office supplies. The AFL-CIO donated typewriters, duplicating machines, a minibus, an offset press, and other supplies requested by Solidarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puddington_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The AFL–CIO sought approval in advance from Solidarity's leadership, to avoid jeopardizing their position with unwanted or surprising American help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnPodhoretz_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-KahnPodhoretz-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHKahn_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-RHKahn-54">[54]</a></sup> On September 12, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lech_Walesa" title="Lech Walesa">Lech Walesa</a> welcomed international donations with this statement: "Help can never be politically embarrassing. That of the AFL-CIO, for example. We are grateful to them. It was a very good thing that they helped us. Whenever we can, we will help them, too."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Kahn explained the AFL–CIO position in a 1981 debate:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"<b>Solidarity made its needs known</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emboldening_added_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emboldening_added-67">[67]</a></sup> with courage, with clarity, and publicly. As you know, <b>the AFL-CIO responded by establishing a fund for the purchase of equipment requested by Solidarity</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emboldening_added_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emboldening_added-67">[67]</a></sup> and we have raised about a quarter of a million dollars for that fund.</p>
<p>This effort has elicited from the Soviet Union, Czechoslovakia, East Germany, and Bulgaria the most massive and vicious propaganda assault on the AFL–CIO ... in many, many years. The ominous tone of the most recent attacks leaves no doubt that <b>if the Soviet Union invades, it shall cite the aid of the AFL-CIO as evidence of outside anti-Socialist intervention</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emboldening_added_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emboldening_added-67">[67]</a></sup> aimed at overthrowing the Polish state. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kahn_2008_234_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kahn_2008_234-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>"All this is by way of introducing the AFL–CIO’s position on economic aid to Poland. In formulating this position, our first concern was to consult our friends in Solidarity .... We did consult with them ... and their views are reflected in the statement unanimously adopted by the AFL–CIO Executive Council.:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>' The AFL-CIO will support additional aid to Poland <b>only if it is conditioned on the adherence of the Polish government to the 21 points of the Gdansk Agreement</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emboldening_added_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emboldening_added-67">[67]</a></sup> Only then could we be assured that the Polish workers will be in a position to defend their gains and to struggle for a fair share of the benefits of Western aid.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
</blockquote>
<p>In testimony to the Joint Congressional Commission on Security and Cooperation in Europe, Kahn suggested policies to support the Polish people, in particular by supporting Solidarity's demand that the Communist regime finally establish legality, by respecting the <a href="/wiki/21_demands_of_MKS" title="21 demands of MKS">twenty-one rights guaranteed by the Polish constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The AFL-CIO provided the most aid to Solidarity, but substantial additional aid was provided by Western-European labor unions, including the U. K.<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> and especially the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="Swedish Trade Union Confederation">Swedish Trade Union Confederation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis32_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis32-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_AFL.E2.80.93CIO">Criticism of AFL–CIO</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Criticism of AFL–CIO">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lkirkland.jpg"><img alt="Portrait of Lane Kirkland" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Lkirkland.jpg/170px-Lkirkland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Lkirkland.jpg/255px-Lkirkland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Lkirkland.jpg/340px-Lkirkland.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lkirkland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
SDUSA leader Tom Kahn was appointed by <a href="/wiki/Lane_Kirkland" title="Lane Kirkland">Lane Kirkland</a> <i>(pictured)</i>, the President of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> after <a href="/wiki/George_Meany" title="George Meany">George Meany</a>, to organize the AFL-CIO's aid to <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity</a>, the Polish labor union that challenged Communism in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis31_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis31-9">[9]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The AFL-CIO's support enraged the Communist regimes of Eastern Europe and the Soviet Union. Its support worried the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carter_Administration" title="Carter Administration">Carter Administration</a>, whose Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Muskie" title="Edmund Muskie">Edmund Muskie</a> told Kirkland that the AFL-CIO's continued support of solidarity could trigger a Soviet invasion of Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis32_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis32-3">[3]</a></sup> After Kirkland refused to withdraw support to Solidarity, Muskie met with the USSR's Ambassador, Anatoly Dobyrnin, to clarify that the AFL-CIO's aid did not have the support of the US government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shevis32_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shevis32-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Aid_through_the_1980s">Aid through the 1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Aid through the 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Later, the <a href="/wiki/National_Endowment_for_Democracy" title="National Endowment for Democracy">National Endowment for Democracy</a> provided $1.7 million for Solidarity, which was transferred via the AFL-CIO. In both 1988 and 1989, the U.S. Congress allocated $1 million yearly to Solidarity via the AFL-CIO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puddington_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-65">[65]</a></sup> In total, the AFL-CIO channeled 4 million dollars to Solidarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puddington_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sandra_Feldman">Sandra Feldman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sandra Feldman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Feldman" title="Sandra Feldman">Sandra Feldman</a></div>
<p>Sandra Feldman (October 13, 1939 – September 18, 2005) was an American civil rights activist, educator and labor leader who served as president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers" title="American Federation of Teachers">American Federation of Teachers</a> (AFT) from 1997 to 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> She helped to organize and was <a class="external text" href="http://www.socialdemocratsusa.org/oldsite/NewApproachesinEducation.html" rel="nofollow">the keynote speaker at the 1999 SDUSA workshop on "American Labor in the New Economy: A Day of Dialogue,"January 22, 1999</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_activism">Socialist activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Socialist activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>She became active in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">socialist</a> politics and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> When she was 17 years old, she met civil rights activist <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a>, who became her mentor and close friend. During her early years in the civil rights movement, Feldman worked to integrate <a href="/wiki/Howard_Johnson%27s" title="Howard Johnson's">Howard Johnson's</a> restaurants in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>. She soon became employment committee chairwoman of the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Racial_Equality" title="Congress of Racial Equality">Congress of Racial Equality</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harlem" title="Harlem">Harlem</a>. She also participated in several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_Rides" title="Freedom Rides">Freedom Rides</a>, and was arrested twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teaching">Teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Upon graduation from <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_College" title="Brooklyn College">Brooklyn College</a> in 1962, Feldman worked for six months as a substitute third-grade teacher in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Harlem" title="Spanish Harlem">East Harlem</a>. She continued to be active in the civil rights movement, working to desegregate Howard Johnson restaurants in Maryland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> She participated in the 1963 <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom</a>, which was organized by Rustin and his associates. From 1963 to 1966, Feldman matriculated in a <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degree</a> program in literature at <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a>. While in graduate school, Feldman worked as a fourth-grade teacher at Public School 34 on the New York City's Lower East Side. She immediately joined the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers" title="American Federation of Teachers">American Federation of Teachers</a>, which had only one other member at the school. When New York City teachers won <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> rights in 1960, she organized the entire school staff within a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> During this time, Feldman became an associate of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Shanker" title="Albert Shanker">Albert Shanker</a>, then an organizer for the <a href="/wiki/United_Federation_of_Teachers" title="United Federation of Teachers">United Federation of Teachers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Federation_of_Teachers_.28UFT.29">United Federation of Teachers (UFT)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United Federation of Teachers (UFT)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Albert_Shanker" title="Albert Shanker">Albert Shanker</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers" title="American Federation of Teachers">American Federation of Teachers</a></div>
<p>In 1966, on the recommendation of Rustin, Shanker—now executive director of the UFT—hired Feldman as a full-time field representative. Over the next nine years, Feldman became the union's executive director and oversaw its staff. She was elected its secretary (the second-most powerful position in the local) in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>After just two years on the UFT staff, Feldman played a crucial role in the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Teachers#Ocean_Hill.E2.80.93Brownsville_strike" title="American Federation of Teachers">Ocean Hill-Brownsville strike</a>. The city of New York had designated the Ocean Hill-Brownsville area of Brooklyn as one of three decentralized school districts in an effort to give the minority community more say in school affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> The crisis began when the Ocean Hill-Brownsville governing board fired 13 teachers for allegedly sabotaging the decentralization experiment. Shanker demanded that specific charges be filed and the teachers given a chance to defend themselves in <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a> proceedings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>A protracted fight erupted between those in the community who supported the Ocean Hill-Brownsville board and those supported the UFT. Many supporters of the local school board resorted to racial invective. Shanker was branded a racist, and many African-Americans accused the UFT of being "Jewish-dominated". Feldman was often at the center of the strike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The UFT emerged from the crisis more powerful than ever, and Feldman's hard work, good political judgment and calm demeanor won her widespread praise within the union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shanker was elected president of the AFT in 1974, but retained his post as president of the UFT. In 1986, Shanker retired as UFT president, and Feldman was elected president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-6"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-7"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UFT_President_after_Shanker">UFT President after Shanker</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: UFT President after Shanker">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Feldman was known for being a quiet but very effective leader of the UFT. She fought school system chancellors and mayors both, winning significantly higher wages and benefits as well as improved working conditions for her members. She lobbied so fiercely for Bernard Gifford as New York City schools chancellor that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_F._Wagner,_Jr._(Deputy_Mayor)" title="Robert F. Wagner, Jr. (Deputy Mayor)">Robert F.
