Wagner, Jr.</a>, President of the New York City Board of Education, threatened to resign unless Feldman backed off and he was given a free hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-7"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-8"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was instrumental in helping <a href="/wiki/David_Dinkins" title="David Dinkins">David Dinkins</a> win election as mayor of New York in 1989 by using union members and resources to build a winning electoral coalition of black and white voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-9"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup> But once mayor, Dinkins stalled on signing a new contract with the teachers' union. Feldman rarely criticized Dinkins publicly for his actions, but she kept the UFT out of Dinkins' 1993 re-election. Dinkins lost in a tight race to <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Giuliani" title="Rudy Giuliani">Rudy Giuliani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-8"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Federation_of_Teachers_.28AFT.29">American Federation of Teachers (AFT)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: American Federation of Teachers (AFT)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Feldman had been elected an AFT vice president in 1974,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> serving on the national union's executive council and the executive council's executive committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-9"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Shanker died in February 1997, Feldman won election as the AFT's president in July 1998, becoming the union's first female president since 1930. Feldman re-emphasized the AFT's commitment to educational issues. She also renewed the union's focus on organizing: During her tenure, the AFT grew by more than 160,000 new members (about 17 percent). With Feldman as President, in 2002, AFT delegates approved a four-point plan: 1) building a "culture of organizing" throughout the union, 2) enhancing the union's political advocacy efforts, 3) engaging in a series of publicity, legislative, funding and political campaigns to strengthen the institutions in which AFT members work, and 4) recommitting the AFT to fostering democratic education and human rights at home and abroad. Feldman moved quickly to ensure that the plan was implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-10"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1997, Feldman was elected to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> executive council and appointed to the executive council's executive committee. During her tenure at the head of the AFT, Feldman also served as a vice president of <a href="/wiki/Education_International" title="Education International">Education International</a> and was a board member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Rescue_Committee" title="International Rescue Committee">International Rescue Committee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-11"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sandra Feldman died in 2005 at the age 65.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_73-12"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeldmanNYT_74-10"><a href="#cite_note-FeldmanNYT-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sidney_Hook">Sidney Hook</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sidney Hook">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sidney_Hook.jpg"><img alt="Picture of Sidney Hook" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="250" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Sidney_Hook.jpg/220px-Sidney_Hook.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/47/Sidney_Hook.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sidney_Hook.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Sidney Hook</a> gave the keynote speech to the second convention of SDUSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hook76_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hook76-47">[47]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Sidney Hook</a></div>
<p>Sidney Hook (December 20, 1902 – July 12, 1989) was an American <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatic</a> philosopher known for his contributions to public debates. A student of <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>, Hook continued to examine the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">philosophy of history</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_education" title="Philosophy of education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">politics</a>, and of <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>. He was known for his criticisms of <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a>. A pragmatic <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democrat</a>, Hook sometimes cooperated with <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservatives</a>, particularly in opposing <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>. After WWII, he argued that members of conspiracies, like the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_USA" title="Communist Party USA">Communist Party USA</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Democratic_centralism" title="Democratic centralism">Leninist conspiracies</a>, ethically could be barred from holding offices of public trust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hook gave the keynote speech to the July 17–18, 1976 convention of SDUSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hook76_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hook76-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>For the Social Democrat, democracy is not merely a political concept but a moral one. It is democracy as a way of life. What is "democracy as a way of life." It is a society whose basic institutions are animated by an equality of concern for all human beings, regardless of class, race, sex, religion, and national origin, to develop themselves as persons to their fullest growth, to be free to live up to their desirable potentials as human beings. It is possible for human beings to be politically equal as voters but yet so unequal in educational, economic, and social opportunities, that ultimately even the nature of their political equality is affected.<br/>
<br/>
When it comes to the principled defense of freedom, and to opposition to all forms of totalitarianism, let it be said that to its eternal credit, the organized labor movement in the United States, in contradiction to all other sectors of American life, especially in industry, the academy and the churches, has never faltered, or trimmed its sails. Its dedication to the ideals of a free society has been unsullied. Its leaders have never been Munich-men of the spirit.<br/>
<br/>
I want to conclude with a few remarks about the domestic scene and the role of Social Democrats, U.S.A. in it. We are not a political party with our own candidates. We are not alone in our specific programs for more employment, more insurance, more welfare, less discrimination, less bureaucratic inefficiency. Our spiritual task should be to relate these programs and demands to the underlying philosophy of democracy, to express and defend those larger moral ideals that should inform, programs for which we wish to develop popular support.<br/>
<br/>
We are few in number and limited in influence. So was the Fabian Society of Great Britain. But in time it reeducated a great political party and much of the nation. We must try to do the same.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penn_Kemble">Penn Kemble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Penn Kemble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Penn_Kemble" title="Penn Kemble">Penn Kemble</a></div>
<p>Richard Penn Kemble (January 21, 1941 — October 15, 2005), commonly known as "Penn," was an American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_activist" title="Political activist">political activist</a> and a founding member of SDUSA. He supported free labor-unions and democracy in the USA and internationally, and so was active in the civil rights movement, the labor movement, and the social-democratic opposition to communism. He founded organizations including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Negotiations_Now!&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Negotiations Now! (page does not exist)">Negotiations Now!</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frontlash" title="Frontlash">Frontlash</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prodemca&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prodemca (page does not exist)">Prodemca</a>. Kemble was appointed to various government boards and institutions throughout the 1990s, eventually becoming the Acting Director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Information_Agency" title="U.S. Information Agency">U.S. Information Agency</a> under President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup> After moving to New York, Kemble stood out as a neatly dressed, muscular Protestant youth, in an urban political setting that was predominantly Catholic and Jewish. He worked at <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> but was fired for refusing to cross a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picketing_(protest)" title="Picketing (protest)">picket line</a> during a typesetters' <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> A leader in the East River chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Racial_Equality" title="Congress of Racial Equality">Congress of Racial Equality</a>, Kemble helped to organize a non-violent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sit_in" title="Sit in">blockade</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Triborough_Bridge" title="Triborough Bridge">Triborough Bridge</a> during rush hour, to raise consciousness among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suburbanite" title="Suburbanite">suburbanites</a> of the lives of Harlem residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> Kemble was a founder of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Negotiation_Now!&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Negotiation Now! (page does not exist)">Negotiation Now!</a>, a group which called for an end to the bombing of <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> and a negotiated settlement of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> He was opposed to a unilateral withdrawal of U.S forces from Vietnam.</p>
<p>In 1972, Kemble was a founder the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_for_a_Democratic_Majority" title="Coalition for a Democratic Majority">Coalition for a Democratic Majority</a> (CDM), an association of centrist Democrats that opposed the "new politics" liberalism exemplified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senator" title="U.S. Senator">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a>, who suffered the worst defeat of a Presidential candidate in modern times, despite the widespread dislike of Nixon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup> Kemble was Executive Director of CDM from 1972–76, at which time he left to become a special assistant and speechwriter for Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Patrick_Moynihan" title="Daniel Patrick Moynihan">Daniel Patrick Moynihan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> He remained with Moynihan until 1979. Concerned about the direct and indirect role of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_USA" title="Communist Party USA">Communist Party USA</a> and of sympathizers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist-Leninist" title="Marxist-Leninist">Marxist-Leninist</a> politics in the US Peace Movement and in the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Churches" title="National Council of Churches">National Council of Churches</a>, Kemble helped found the <a href="/wiki/Institute_on_Religion_and_Democracy" title="Institute on Religion and Democracy">Institute on Religion and Democracy</a>. From 1981 until 1988 Kemble was the President of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Friends_of_the_Democratic_Center_in_Central_America&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Friends of the Democratic Center in Central America (page does not exist)">Committee for Democracy in Central America</a> (PRODEMCA), which opposed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sandinista" title="Sandinista">Sandinistas</a> and related groups in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>He supported the Bill Clinton's campaign for the Presidency. During the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Presidency of Bill Clinton">Presidency of Bill Clinton</a>, Kemble served first in 1993 as the Deputy Director and then in 1999 as Acting Director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Information_Agency" title="U.S. Information Agency">U.S. Information Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup> He was also made a special representative of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madeleine_K._Albright" title="Madeleine K. Albright">Madeleine K. Albright</a> to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Community_of_Democracies_Initiative&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Community of Democracies Initiative (page does not exist)">Community of Democracies Initiative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoSD_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-NeoSD-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, Kemble was appointed to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Board_of_International_Broadcasting&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Board of International Broadcasting (page does not exist)">Board of International Broadcasting</a> by <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">President</a> <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup> He also became the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> representative of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>; in his last years, he was especially involved in supporting peace efforts in the Middle East. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colin_L._Powell" title="Colin L. Powell">Colin L. Powell</a> appointed Kemble to be the Chairman of the International Eminent Persons Group on Slavery, Abduction and Forced Servitude in <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Sudan" title="Slavery in Sudan">Sudan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-times-78">[78]</a></sup> Despite being diagnosed with brain cancer, Kemble spent his last months organizing a conference on the contributions of <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Sidney Hook</a>, the late pragmatic philosopher and SDUSA spokesperson; Carl Gershman took over the leadership of the conference after Kemble's cancer made it impossible for him to continue.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carl_Gershman">Carl Gershman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Carl Gershman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gershman" title="Carl Gershman">Carl Gershman</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carl_Gershman" title="Carl Gershman">Carl Gershman</a> was the Executive Director of the SDUSA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RustinGershman_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-RustinGershman-49">[49]</a></sup> from 1975 to 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reed-80">[80]</a></sup> After having served as the U.S. Representative to the U.N.'s Committee on human rights during the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" title="Reagan Administration">Reagan Administration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lip34_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lip34-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nossiter_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nossiter-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gershman" title="Carl Gershman">Carl Gershman</a> has served as the President of the <a href="/wiki/National_Endowment_for_Democracy" title="National Endowment for Democracy">National Endowment for Democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NED_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-NED-83">[83]</a></sup> After the Polish people overthrew communism, their elected government awarded the Order of the Knight's Cross to Carl Gershman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NED_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-NED-83">[83]</a></sup> and (posthumously) the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_White_Eagle_(Poland)" title="Order of the White Eagle (Poland)">Order of the White Eagle</a> to AFL-CIO President <a href="/wiki/Lane_Kirkland" title="Lane Kirkland">Lane Kirkland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hiatus_and_re-foundation">Hiatus and re-foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hiatus and re-foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Following the death of the organization's <i>Notesonline</i> editor <a href="/wiki/Penn_Kemble" title="Penn Kemble">Penn Kemble</a> of cancer on 15 October 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Social Democrats USA lapsed into a state of organizational hiatus, with no further issues of the online newsletter produced or updates to the group's website made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following several years of inactivity, an attempt was subsequently made to revive Social Democrats, USA. In 2008, a group composed initially mostly of Pennsylvania members of SDUSA emerged, determined to re-launch the organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hacker_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hacker-87">[87]</a></sup> A re-founding convention of the Social Democrats, USA was held May 3, 2009, at which a National Executive Committee was elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Owing to factional disagreements, a group based in Johnstown, Pennsylvania and the newly elected NEC parted company, with the former styling itself as the "Social Democrats USA — Socialist Party, USA"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and the latter as "Social Democrats, USA."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two additional conventions took place since the 2009 reformation, an internet <a href="/wiki/Teleconference" title="Teleconference">teleconference</a> on September 1, 2010 featuring presentations by guest speakers Herb Engstrom of the California Democratic Party Executive Committee, and Roger Clayman, Executive Director of the Long Island Labor Federation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and a convention held August 26–27, 2012 in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a> with a keynote address delivered by Richard Lipsitz, Executive Director of Western New York Labor Federation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-Communism">Anti-Communism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Anti-Communism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Michael Harrington charged that its "obsessive anti-Communism" rendered SDUSA politically conservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloodworth148_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloodworth148-93">[93]</a></sup> In contrast, Harrington's DSOC and DSA criticized Communism but opposed many defense-and-diplomatic policies against the Soviet Union and its <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a>. Harrington voiced admiration for German Chancellor Willy Brandt's <i><a href="/wiki/Ostpolitik" title="Ostpolitik">Ostpolitik</a></i>, which sought to reduce Western distrust of and hostility towards the Eastern Bloc and so entice the USSR reciprocally to reduce its aggressive military posture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> A member of DSA's National Committee, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurt_Stand" title="Kurt Stand">Kurt Stand</a>, was convicted of spying on behalf of the East German secret-police (<a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a>) for 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahler_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mahler-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Max_Shachtman_and_alleged_Trotskyism">Max Shachtman and alleged Trotskyism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Max Shachtman and alleged Trotskyism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>SDUSA leaders have served in the administrations of US presidents since the 1980; the service of some members in Republican administrations has been associated with controversy. SDUSA members like Gershman were called "State Department socialists" by <a href="#CITEREFMassing1987">Massing (1987)</a>, who wrote that the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_Ronald_Reagan_administration" title="Foreign policy of the Ronald Reagan administration">foreign policy of the Reagan administration</a> was being run by <a href="/wiki/Trotskyism" title="Trotskyism">Trotskyists</a>, a claim that was called a "myth" by <a href="#CITEREFLipset1988">Lipset (1988</a>, p. 34).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lip34_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lip34-81">[81]</a></sup> This "Trotskyist" charge has been repeated and even widened by journalist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lind" title="Michael Lind">Michael Lind</a> in 2003 to assert a takeover of the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_George_W._Bush_administration" title="Foreign policy of the George W. Bush administration">foreign policy of the George W. Bush administration</a> by former Trotskyists;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Lind's "amalgamation of the defense intellectuals with the traditions and theories of 'the largely Jewish-American Trotskyist movement' [in Lind's words]" was criticized in 2003 by University of Michigan professor Alan M. Wald,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> who had discussed Trotskyism in his history of "<a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Intellectuals" title="The New York Intellectuals">the New York intellectuals</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> SDUSA and allegations that "Trotskyists" subverted Bush's foreign policy have been mentioned by "self-styled" <a href="/wiki/Paleoconservatism" title="Paleoconservatism">paleoconservatives</a> (<a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> opponents of <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neoconservatism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harrington and Tom Kahn had been associated with <a href="/wiki/Max_Shachtman" title="Max Shachtman">Max Shachtman</a>, a Marxist theorist, who had broken with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trotsky" title="Trotsky">Trotsky</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> because of his criticism of the Soviet Union as being a totalitarian class-society after having supported Trotsky in the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Although Max Schachtman died in 1972 before the Socialist Party was renamed as SDUSA, Shachtman's ideas continued to influence the Albert Shanker and The American Federation of Teachers, which was often associated with SDUSA members. Decades later, conflicts in the AFL-CIO were roughly split along the lines of the conflict between the "Shachtmanite Social Democrats and the Harringtonite Democratic Socialists of America, with the Social Democrats supporting Kirkland and Donahue and the Democratic Socialists supporting Sweeney",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_conservatism_or_neoconservatism">Alleged conservatism or neoconservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Alleged conservatism or neoconservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some SDUSA members have been called right-wing social-democrats,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaisse91_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vaisse91-5">[5]</a></sup> a taunt according to Wattenberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wattenberg_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wattenberg-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>SDUSA members supported the independent labor-union of Poland, Solidarity. The organizer of the AFL-CIO's support for Solidarity, SDUSA's Tom Kahn, criticized <a href="/wiki/Jeane_Kirkpatrick" title="Jeane Kirkpatrick">Jeane Kirkpatrick</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dictatorship_and_Double_Standards" title="Dictatorship and Double Standards">Dictatorship and Double Standards</a></i>, arguing that <a href="/wiki/Democracy_promotion" title="Democracy promotion">democracy be promoted</a> even in the countries dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_communism" title="Soviet communism">Soviet communism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnDoubleStandards_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-KahnDoubleStandards-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1981 leading Social Democrats and some moderate Republicans wanted to use economic aid to Poland as leverage to expand the freedom of association in 1981, whereas Casper Weinberger and neoconservative Jeane Kirkpatrick preferred to force the Communist government of Poland to default on its international payments and so lose credibility;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Kahn argued for his position in a 1981 debate with neoconservative Norman Podhoretz, who like Kirkpatrick and Weinberger opposed all credits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahnPodhoretz_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-KahnPodhoretz-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG11_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-CG11-12">[12]</a></sup> In 1982 Kirkpatrick called similarly for Western assistance to Poland to be used to help Solidarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of SDUSA's former members have been called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoconservative" title="Neoconservative">neoconservatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Justin Vaisse listed five SDUSA associates as "second-generation neoconservatives" and "so-called Shachtmanites", including "Penn Kemble, Joshua Muravchik, ..., and Bayard Rustin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaisse2ndGen_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaisse2ndGen-115">[115]</a></sup> Throughout his life, <a href="/wiki/Penn_Kemble" title="Penn Kemble">Penn Kemble</a> called himself a social-democrat and objected to being called a neoconservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup> Kemble and <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Muravchik" title="Joshua Muravchik">Joshua Muravchik</a> were never followers of Max Shachtman; on the contrary, Kemble was recruited by a non-Shachtmanite professor, according to Muravchik, who wrote, "Although Shachtman was one of the elder statesmen who occasionally made stirring speeches to us, no YPSL [Young People's Socialist League] of my generation was a Shachtmanite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuravchikComrades_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-MuravchikComrades-20">[20]</a></sup> Besides objecting to being called a "neoconservative", Kemble "sharply criticized the Bush administration's approach on [Iraq]. 'The distinction between liberation and democratization, which requires a strategy and instruments, was an idea never understood by the administration,' he told the <i>New Republic</i>", wrote the <i>Washington Post</i> in Kemble's obituary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_member_Joshua_Muravchik">Former member Joshua Muravchik</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Former member Joshua Muravchik">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Joshua Muravchik has identified himself as a neoconservative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MuravchikWeNeoconservatives_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-MuravchikWeNeoconservatives-19">[19]</a></sup> When Muravhchik appeared at the 2003 SDUSA conference, he was criticized by SDUSA members:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeyersonDissent_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-MeyersonDissent-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Rachelle Horowitz, another Social Democrats, USA, luminary and an event organizer, called Muravchik’s comments “profoundly disturbing” — both his use of “us and them” rhetoric and the term “evil.” The existence of evil in the world was something Horowitz was happy to concede, she said from the floor. But it was a word incapable of clear political definition and thus a producer of muddle rather than clarity, zeal rather than political action.</p>
<p>Then Herf jumped in with similar criticisms. And then Berman. And Ibrahim. And before long, more or less everyone else in the room. There was still something, it seemed, that separated them from the neocons who hovered over the proceedings both as opponents and inspirations. Muravchik wanted to pull them somewhere most of the attendees — and organizers — were unwilling to go."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Among Joshua Muravchick's SDUSA citics was his own father, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Emanuel_Muravchik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Emanuel Muravchik (page does not exist)">Emanuel Muravchik</a> (a Norman Thomas Socialist);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeyersonDissent_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-MeyersonDissent-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revisits_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-revisits-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manny_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manny-117">[117]</a></sup> his mother was too upset with Joshua's <i>Heaven on Earth: The Rise and Fall of Socialism</i> to attend the discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revisits_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-revisits-116">[116]</a></sup> On the other hand, Joshua Muravchik was called a "second-generation neoconservative" by Vaisse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaisse2ndGen_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaisse2ndGen-115">[115]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-NYTimes-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NYTimes_1-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news" id="CITEREFAnonymous1972">Anonymous (31 December 1972). <a class="external text" href="http://select.nytimes.com/gst/abstract.html?res=F00B16FC3E5A137A93C3AA1789D95F468785F9" rel="nofollow">"Socialist Party now the Social Democrats, U.S.A."</a>. <i>New York Times</i>. p. 36<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Bloodworth147-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bloodworth147_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBloodworth2013">Bloodworth (2013</a>, p. 147)</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FPTP-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FPTP_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFRustin1965">Rustin, Bayard (February 1965). <a class="external text" href="http://www.commentarymagazine.com/article/from-protest-to-politics-the-future-of-the-civil-rights-movement/" rel="nofollow">"From protest to politics: The future of the civil rights movement"</a>. <i>Commentary</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=From+protest+to+politics%3A+The+future+of+the+civil+rights+movement&amp;rft.aufirst=Bayard&amp;rft.aulast=Rustin&amp;rft.date=1965-02&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.commentarymagazine.com%2Farticle%2Ffrom-protest-to-politics-the-future-of-the-civil-rights-movement%2F&amp;rft.jtitle=Commentary&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-RKRustin-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-RKRustin_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RKRustin_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFKennedy2003">Kennedy, Randall (29 September 2003). <a class="external text" href="http://www.thenation.com/article/protest-patronage" rel="nofollow">"From protest to patronage"</a>. <i>The Nation</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=From+protest+to+patronage&amp;rft.aufirst=Randall&amp;rft.aulast=Kennedy&amp;rft.date=2003-09-29&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thenation.com%2Farticle%2Fprotest-patronage&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Nation&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wilson's <i>The Declining Significance of Race</i> won the <a href="/wiki/American_Sociological_Association" title="American Sociological Association">American Sociological Association</a>'s <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sydney_Spivack_Award&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sydney Spivack Award (page does not exist)">Sydney Spivack Award</a>. In <i>The Declining Significance of Race: Blacks and Changing American Institutions</i> (1978) <a href="/wiki/Split_labor_market_theory" title="Split labor market theory">Wilson</a> argues that the significance of race is waning, and an African-American's class is comparatively more important in determining his or her life chances. His <i>The Truly Disadvantaged</i>, which was selected by the editors of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> Book Review as one of the 16 best books of 1987, and received <i>The Washington Monthly</i> Annual Book Award and the Society for the Study of Social Problems' C. Wright Mills Award. In <i>The Truly Disadvantaged: The Inner City, the Underclass, and Public Policy</i> (1987), Wilson was one of the first to enunciate at length the "<a href="/wiki/Spatial_mismatch" title="Spatial mismatch">spatial mismatch</a>" theory for the development of a ghetto underclass. As industrial jobs disappeared in cities in the wake of global <a href="/wiki/Economic_restructuring" title="Economic restructuring">economic restructuring</a>, and hence urban <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> increased, women found it unwise to marry the fathers of their children, since the fathers would not be breadwinners. His <i><a href="/wiki/When_Work_Disappears" title="When Work Disappears">When Work Disappears</a>: The World of the New Urban Poor</i>, which was selected as one of the notable books of 1996 by the editors of the New York Times Book Review and received the Sidney Hillman Foundation Award. His <i>The Bridge Over the Racial Divide: Rising Inequality and Coalition Politics</i> reaffirms the need for a coalition strategy, as Rustin suggested. In Wilson's most recent book, <i>More Than Just Race: Being Black and Poor in the Inner City</i> (2009), he directs his attention to the overall framing of pervasive, concentrated urban poverty of African Americans. He asks the question, "Why do poverty and unequal opportunity persist in the lives of so many African Americans?" In response, he traces the history and current state of powerful structural factors impacting African Americans, such as discrimination in laws, policies, hiring, housing, and education. Wilson also examines the interplay of structural factors and the attitudes and assumptions of African Americans, European Americans, and social science researchers. In identifying the dynamic influence of structural, economic, and cultural factors, he argues against either/or politicized views of poverty among African Americans that either focus blame solely on cultural factors or only on unjust structural factors. He tries "to demonstrate the importance of understanding not only the independent contributions of social structure and culture, but also how they interact to shape different group outcomes that embody racial inequality." Wilson's goal is to "rethink the way we talk about addressing the problems of race and urban poverty in the public policy arena." <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.prrac.org/pdf/WJWMayJune2009PRRAC.pdf" rel="nofollow">[1]</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Kahn, Tom (March 3, 1982). "Moral duty". <i>Society</i>. New York: Transactions Publishers (purchased by Springer). <b>19</b> (3): 51. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1007%2FBF02698967" rel="nofollow">10.1007/BF02698967</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0147-2011" rel="nofollow">0147-2011</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=Moral+duty&amp;rft.aufirst=Tom&amp;rft.aulast=Kahn&amp;rft.date=1982-03-03&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2FBF02698967&amp;rft.issn=0147-2011&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Society&amp;rft.pages=51&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=19"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPuddington2008">Puddington (2008)</a> wrote:</span>
<blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text">"Kirkland's embrace of Solidarity brought him into immediate conflict with the Carter administration. Despite the administration's avowed commitment to human rights, Edmund Muskie, secretary of state, decided that quiet diplomacy was the most prudent course to follow in the Polish crisis. He summoned Kirkland to his office for lunch on September 3, 1980, during which he gave a 'negative assessment' of the Polish aid fund that the AFL-CIO had just launched and declared that the federation's open support for Solidarity could be 'deliberately misinterpreted' by the Kremlin in order to justify military intervention. Muskie was not alone in deploring labor's Polish initiative. In a <i>New York Times</i> column, Flora Lewis called the Workers Aid Fund 'most unfortunate.' <a href="/wiki/Flora_Lewis" title="Flora Lewis">Flora Lewis</a>, "Let the Poles Do It," <i>New York Times</i>, September 5, 1980.]"</span></p>
</blockquote>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Isserman, <i>The Other American</i>, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.se/books?id=Ym-qm7i5WHYC" rel="nofollow">pp. 351–352</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As co-chairman of DSA, Michael Harrington wrote that Willy Brandt</span>
<blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text">"launched his famous <i>ostpolitik</i> (Eastern policy), and moved toward detente with the Soviets and Eastern Europeans—a strategy that was to win him the Nobel Peace Prize."</span></p>
<p><span class="reference-text">"Disaster came in 1974. There was a spy scandal—a member of Brandt's inner circle turned out to be an East German agent—and the chancellor resigned his office."</span></p>
</blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news" id="CITEREFHarrington1987"><a href="/wiki/Michael_Harrington" title="Michael Harrington">Harrington, Michael</a> (March 31, 1987). <a class="external text" href="http://articles.latimes.com/1987-03-31/local/me-1327_1_willy-brandt" rel="nofollow">"Willy Brandt May Even Yet Manage Resurrection No. 5"</a>. <i>Los Angeles Times</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=Willy+Brandt+May+Even+Yet+Manage+Resurrection+No.+5&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.aulast=Harrington&amp;rft.date=1987-03-31&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Farticles.latimes.com%2F1987-03-31%2Flocal%2Fme-1327_1_willy-brandt&amp;rft.jtitle=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></p>

<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFWald1987">Wald, Alan M. <p><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFKing2004">King, Bill (March 22, 2004). <a class="external text" href="http://www.enterstageright.com/archive/articles/0304/0304neocontrotp1.htm" rel="nofollow">"Neoconservatives and Trotskyism"</a>. <i>Enter Stage Right: Politics, Culture, Economics</i> (3): 1 2. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1488-1756" rel="nofollow">1488-1756</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=Neoconservatives+and+Trotskyism&amp;rft.aufirst=Bill&amp;rft.aulast=King&amp;rft.date=2004-03-22&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.enterstageright.com%2Farchive%2Farticles%2F0304%2F0304neocontrotp1.htm&amp;rft.issn=1488-1756&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Enter+Stage+Right%3A+Politics%2C+Culture%2C+Economics&amp;rft.pages=1-2&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="error citation-comment" style="font-size:100%"><code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|chapter=</code> ignored (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#chapter_ignored" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></p>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMuravchik2006">Muravchik (2006)</a>. Addressing the allegation that SDUSUA was a "Trotskyist" organization, Muravchik wrote that in the early 1960s, two future members of SDUSA, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kahn" title="Tom Kahn">Tom Kahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Feldman" title="Paul Feldman">Paul Feldman</a></span>
<blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text">"became devotees of a former Trotskyist named Max Shachtman—a fact that today has taken on a life of its own. Tracing forward in lineage through me and a few other ex-YPSL’s [members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_Peoples_Socialist_League" title="Young Peoples Socialist League">Young Peoples Socialist League</a>] turned neoconservatives, this happenstance has fueled the accusation that neoconservatism itself, and through it the foreign policy of the Bush administration, are somehow rooted in 'Trotskyism.'</span></p>
<p><span class="reference-text">I am more inclined to laugh than to cry over this, but since the myth has traveled so far, let me briefly try once more, as I have done at greater length in the past, to set the record straight.[See "The Neoconservative Cabal," <i>Commentary</i>, September 2003] The alleged connective chain is broken at every link. The falsity of its more recent elements is readily ascertainable by anyone who cares for the truth—namely, that George Bush was never a neoconservative and that most neoconservatives were never YPSL’s. The earlier connections are more obscure but no less false. Although Shachtman was one of the elder statesmen who occasionally made stirring speeches to us, no YPSL of my generation was a Shachtmanite. What is more, our mentors, Paul and Tom, had come under Shachtman’s sway years after he himself had ceased to be a Trotskyite.</span></p>
</blockquote>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Publications">Publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Democrats,_USA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFSocial_Democrats.2C_USA1972">Social Democrats, USA (December 1972) [copyright 1973]. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/TheAmericanChallengeASocial-democraticProgramForTheSeventies" rel="nofollow"><i>The American challenge: A social-democratic program for the seventies</i></a>. <li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFHook2005"><a href="/wiki/Sidney_Hook" title="Sidney Hook">Hook, Sidney</a> (2005) [17–18 July 1976], originally entitled "The social  democratic prospect: Social democracy and America", <a class="external text" href="http://dissentmagazine.org/democratiya/docs/d3Whole.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The social democratic prospect"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Democratiya" title="Democratiya">Democratiya</a>,</i> archived by <i><a href="/wiki/Dissent_(American_magazine)" title="Dissent (American magazine)">Dissent</a></i>, originally New York: Social Democrats, USA, <b>3</b> (Winter): 63–76, <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/TheSocialDemocraticProspect" rel="nofollow">Alternative source</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASocial+Democrats%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=The+social+democratic+prospect&amp;rft.aufirst=Sidney&amp;rft.aulast=Hook&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdissentmagazine.org%2Fdemocratiya%2Fdocs%2Fd3Whole.pdf&amp;rft.issue=Winter&amp;rft.jtitle=Democratiya%2C+%27%27archived+by+%27%27Dissent&amp;rft.pages=63-76&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=3"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">Precursors</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
